<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Middle_east.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Middle_east.jpg/260px-Middle_east.jpg" width="260" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Middle_east.jpg/390px-Middle_east.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Middle_east.jpg/520px-Middle_east.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1005" data-file-height="938" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Middle_east.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Middle East between Africa, Europe, and Central Asia.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Middle_East_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Middle_East_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/260px-Middle_East_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png" width="260" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Middle_East_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/390px-Middle_East_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Middle_East_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/520px-Middle_East_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1900" data-file-height="1900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Middle_East_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Middle East map of Köppen climate classification.</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Middle East</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Transcontinental_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Transcontinental country">transcontinental</a> <a href="/wiki/Region" title="Region">region</a> centered on <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> (both Asian and <a href="/wiki/East_Thrace" title="East Thrace">European</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> (which is mostly in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>). The corresponding adjective is <i>Middle Eastern</i> and the derived noun is <i>Middle Easterner</i>. The term has come into wider usage as a replacement of the term <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> (as opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a>) beginning in the early 20th century.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijanis" title="Azerbaijanis">Azeris</a> (excluding <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>) constitute the largest ethnic groups in the region by population.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Indigenous minorities of the Middle East include <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baloch_people" title="Baloch people">Baloch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arameans_in_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="Arameans in Syria">Arameans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a> (who primarily live in North Africa), <a href="/wiki/Copts" title="Copts">Copts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lurs" title="Lurs">Lurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandaeans" title="Mandaeans">Mandaeans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samaritans" title="Samaritans">Samaritans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shabak_people" title="Shabak people">Shabaks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tat_people_(Iran)" title="Tat people (Iran)">Tats</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zazas" title="Zazas">Zazas</a>. In the Middle East, there is also a <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Romani</a> community. European ethnic groups that form a diaspora in the region include <a href="/wiki/Albanians" title="Albanians">Albanians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bosniaks" title="Bosniaks">Bosniaks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Kabardians" title="Kabardians">Kabardians</a>), <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Tatars" title="Crimean Tatars">Crimean Tatars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levantines_(Latin_Christians)" class="mw-redirect" title="Levantines (Latin Christians)">Franco-Levantines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Levantines_(Latin_Christians)#Italian_Levantines" class="mw-redirect" title="Levantines (Latin Christians)">Italo-Levantines</a>. Among other migrant populations are <a href="/wiki/Bengalis" title="Bengalis">Bengalis</a> as well as other <a href="/wiki/Indians_in_Saudi_Arabia" title="Indians in Saudi Arabia">Indians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people_in_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="Chinese people in the United Arab Emirates">Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Filipinos_in_Saudi_Arabia" title="Filipinos in Saudi Arabia">Filipinos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesians_in_Saudi_Arabia" title="Indonesians in Saudi Arabia">Indonesians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistanis" title="Pakistanis">Pakistanis</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Afghans" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghans">Afghans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africans</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Middle_East" title="History of the Middle East">history of the Middle East</a> dates back to ancient times, with the (geopolitical) importance of the region being recognized for millennia.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Several major religions have their origins in the Middle East, including <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Baha%27i_faith" class="mw-redirect" title="Baha&#39;i faith">Baha'i faith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandaeism" title="Mandaeism">Mandaeism</a>, Unitarian <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a>, and numerous other belief systems were also established within the region.
</p><p>The Middle East generally has a hot, <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> climate, with several major rivers providing <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> to support <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> in limited areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a> in Egypt, the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> watersheds of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, and most of what is known as the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>.
</p><p>Most of the countries that border the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> have vast reserves of <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>, with monarchs of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> in particular benefiting economically from petroleum exports.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<p>The term "Middle East" may have originated in the 1850s in the British <a href="/wiki/India_Office" title="India Office">India Office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaumontBlakeWagstaff198816_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaumontBlakeWagstaff198816-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> However, it became more widely known when <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> naval strategist <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Thayer_Mahan" title="Alfred Thayer Mahan">Alfred Thayer Mahan</a> used the term in 1902<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> to "designate the area between Arabia and India".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> During this time the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empires</a> were vying for influence in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, a rivalry which would become known as <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Game" title="The Great Game">The Great Game</a>. Mahan realized not only the strategic importance of the region, but also of its center, the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>  He labeled the area surrounding the Persian Gulf as the Middle East, and said that after Egypt's <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>, it was the most important passage for Britain to control in order to keep the Russians from advancing towards <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>  Mahan first used the term in his article "The Persian Gulf and International Relations", published in September 1902 in the <i><a href="/wiki/National_Review_(London)" title="National Review (London)">National Review</a></i>, a British journal.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> The Middle East, if I may adopt a term which I have not seen, will some day need <a href="/wiki/Malta_Protectorate" title="Malta Protectorate">its Malta</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Gibraltar" title="Capture of Gibraltar">its Gibraltar</a>; it does not follow that either will be in the Persian Gulf.  Naval force has the quality of mobility which carries with it the privilege of temporary absences; but it needs to find on every scene of operation established bases of refit, of supply, and in case of disaster, of security. The British Navy should have the facility to concentrate in force if occasion arise, about <a href="/wiki/Aden_Province" title="Aden Province">Aden</a>, India, and the Persian Gulf.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdelson199522–23_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdelson199522–23-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Mahan's article was reprinted in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> and followed in October by a 20-article series entitled "The Middle Eastern Question," written by Sir <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_Valentine_Chirol" title="Ignatius Valentine Chirol">Ignatius Valentine Chirol</a>. During this series, Sir Ignatius expanded the definition of <i>Middle East</i> to include "those regions of Asia which extend to the borders of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> or command the approaches to India."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdelson199524_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdelson199524-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> After the series ended in 1903, <i>The Times</i> removed quotation marks from subsequent uses of the term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdelson199526_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdelson199526-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, it was customary to refer to areas centered around <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and the eastern shore of the Mediterranean as the "<a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>", while the "<a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a>" centered on <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-davison_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davison-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and the Middle East then meant the area from <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma">Burma</a>, namely the area between the Near East and the Far East.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In the late 1930s, the British established the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East_Command" title="Middle East Command">Middle East Command</a>, which was based in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, for its military forces in the region. After that time, the term "Middle East" gained broader usage in Europe and the United States, with the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East_Institute" title="Middle East Institute">Middle East Institute</a> founded in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> in 1946, among other usage.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_and_usage">Criticism and usage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:400px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Low bandwidth VP9 (180P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;VP9 180P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;180p.vp9.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;240&quot; data-height=&quot;240&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;327896&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Middle East.ogv" style="width:220px;height:165px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Middle_East.ogv/220px--Middle_East.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/44/Middle_East.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Middle_East.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1957 American film about the Middle East</div></div></div>
<p>The description <i>Middle</i> has also led to some confusion over changing definitions. Before the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>, "Near East" was used in English to refer to the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, while "Middle East" referred to <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, Central Asia, and <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a>. In contrast, "Far East" referred to the countries of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, etc.)
</p><p>With the disappearance of the Ottoman Empire in 1918, "Near East" largely fell out of common use in English, while "Middle East" came to be applied to the re-emerging countries of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a>. However, the usage "Near East" was retained by a variety of academic disciplines, including <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">ancient history</a>, where it describes an area identical to the term <i>Middle East</i>, which is not used by these disciplines (see <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a>).
</p><p>The first official use of the term "Middle East" by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">United States government</a> was in the 1957 <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Doctrine" title="Eisenhower Doctrine">Eisenhower Doctrine</a>, which pertained to the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>. Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/John_Foster_Dulles" title="John Foster Dulles">John Foster Dulles</a> defined the Middle East as "the area lying between and including <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> on the west and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> on the east, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> on the North and the Arabian peninsula to the south, plus the <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-davison_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davison-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In 1958, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State Department</a> explained that the terms "Near East" and "Middle East" were interchangeable, and defined the region as including only <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> Stylebook says that Near East formerly referred to the farther west countries while Middle East referred to the eastern ones, but that now they are synonymous. It instructs:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Use <i>Middle East</i> unless <i>Near East</i> is used by a source in a story. <i>Mideast</i> is also acceptable, but <i>Middle East</i> is preferred.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The term <i>Middle East</i> has also been criticised as <a href="/wiki/Eurocentric" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurocentric">Eurocentric</a> ("based on a British Western perception") by Hanafi (1998).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Translations">Translations</span></h3>
<p>There are terms similar to <i>Near East</i> and <i>Middle East</i> in other European languages, but since it is a relative description, the meanings depend on the country and are different from the English terms generally. In <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> the term <i><a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naher_Osten" class="extiw" title="de:Naher Osten">Naher Osten</a></i> (Near East) is still in common use (nowadays the term <i>Mittlerer Osten</i> is more and more common in press texts translated from English sources, albeit having a distinct meaning) and in <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> <a href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%91%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%B6%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%92%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BA" class="extiw" title="ru:Ближний Восток">Ближний Восток</a> or <i>Blizhniy Vostok</i>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a> <a href="https://bg.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%91%D0%BB%D0%B8%D0%B7%D1%8A%D0%BA_%D0%98%D0%B7%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BA" class="extiw" title="bg:Близък Изток">Близкия Изток</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> <i><a href="https://pl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bliski_Wsch%C3%B3d" class="extiw" title="pl:Bliski Wschód">Bliski Wschód</a></i> or <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">Croatian</a> <i><a href="https://hr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bliski_istok" class="extiw" title="hr:Bliski istok">Bliski istok</a></i> (meaning <i>Near East</i> in all the four Slavic languages) remains as the only appropriate term for the region. However, some languages do have "Middle East" equivalents, such as the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moyen-Orient" class="extiw" title="fr:Moyen-Orient">Moyen-Orient</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a> <a href="https://sv.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mellan%C3%B6stern" class="extiw" title="sv:Mellanöstern">Mellanöstern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> <a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oriente_Medio" class="extiw" title="es:Oriente Medio">Oriente Medio or Medio Oriente</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> <a href="https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medio_Oriente" class="extiw" title="it:Medio Oriente">Medio Oriente</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Perhaps because of the influence of the Western press, the Arabic equivalent of <i>Middle East</i> (Arabic: الشرق الأوسط  <i>ash-Sharq al-Awsaṭ</i>), has become standard usage in the mainstream Arabic press, comprehending the same meaning as the term "Middle East" in North American and Western European usage. The designation, <i><a href="/wiki/Mashriq" title="Mashriq">Mashriq</a></i>, also from the Arabic root for <i>East</i>, also denotes a variously defined region around the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, the eastern part of the Arabic-speaking world (as opposed to the <i><a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a></i>, the western part).<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Even though the term originated in the West, apart from Arabic, other languages of countries of the Middle East also use a translation of it. The <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> equivalent for Middle East is خاورمیانه (<i>Khāvar-e miyāneh</i>), the Hebrew is המזרח התיכון (<i>hamizrach hatikhon</i>) and the Turkish is Orta Doğu.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Territories_and_regions">Territories and regions</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For a more comprehensive list, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_Middle_Eastern_countries_by_population" title="List of Middle Eastern countries by population">List of Middle Eastern countries by population</a>.</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territories_and_regions_usually_considered_within_the_Middle_East">Territories and regions usually considered within the Middle East</span></h3>
<p>Traditionally included within the Middle East are <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (Persia), <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. In modern-day-country terms they are these:
</p>

<p><small>
</small></p><small><dl><dd>a. <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_israel"><b><a href="#ref_israel">^</a></b></span> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_palestine"><b><a href="#ref_palestine">^</a></b></span> <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_Law" title="Jerusalem Law">proclaimed capital of Israel</a> and the actual location of the <a href="/wiki/Knesset" title="Knesset">Knesset</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israeli_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Israeli Supreme Court">Israeli Supreme Court</a>, and other governmental institutions of Israel. <a href="/wiki/Ramallah" title="Ramallah">Ramallah</a> is the actual location of the government of Palestine, whereas the proclaimed capital of Palestine is <a href="/wiki/East_Jerusalem" title="East Jerusalem">East Jerusalem</a>, which is <a href="/wiki/Positions_on_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Positions on Jerusalem">disputed</a>.</dd>

Seat of government moved to Aden.</dd></dl></small><small></small><p><small></small>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_definitions_of_the_Middle_East">Other definitions of the Middle East</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greater_Middle_East" title="Greater Middle East">Greater Middle East</a></div>
<p>Various concepts are often being paralleled to Middle East, most notably Near East, <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a> and the Levant. Near East, Levant and Fertile Crescent are geographic concepts, which refer to large sections of the modern defined Middle East, with Near East being the closest to Middle East in its geographic meaning. Due to it primarily being Arabic speaking, the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> region of North Africa is sometimes included.
</p><p>The countries of the <a href="/wiki/South_Caucasus" class="mw-redirect" title="South Caucasus">South Caucasus</a>—<a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>—are occasionally included in definitions of the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Greater_Middle_East" title="Greater Middle East">Greater Middle East</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Political_geography" title="Political geography">political</a> term coined by the <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush_administration" class="mw-redirect" title="George W. Bush administration">second Bush administration</a> in the first decade of the 21st century,<sup id="cite_ref-haeri_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haeri-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> to denote various countries, pertaining to the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>, specifically <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Various <a href="/wiki/Central_Asian" class="mw-redirect" title="Central Asian">Central Asian</a> countries are sometimes also included.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Middle_East" title="History of the Middle East">History of the Middle East</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_modern_conflicts_in_the_Middle_East" title="List of modern conflicts in the Middle East">List of modern conflicts in the Middle East</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Middle_East&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(December 2011)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jerusalem_kotel_mosque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Jerusalem_kotel_mosque.jpg/170px-Jerusalem_kotel_mosque.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Jerusalem_kotel_mosque.jpg/255px-Jerusalem_kotel_mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/Jerusalem_kotel_mosque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="270" data-file-height="359" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jerusalem_kotel_mosque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Western_Wall" title="Western Wall">Western Wall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dome_of_the_Rock" title="Dome of the Rock">Dome of the Rock</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaaba_mirror_edit_jj.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Kaaba_mirror_edit_jj.jpg/220px-Kaaba_mirror_edit_jj.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Kaaba_mirror_edit_jj.jpg/330px-Kaaba_mirror_edit_jj.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Kaaba_mirror_edit_jj.jpg/440px-Kaaba_mirror_edit_jj.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaaba_mirror_edit_jj.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Middle East lies at the juncture of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>. It is the birthplace and <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> center of religions such as <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yezidi" class="mw-redirect" title="Yezidi">Yezidi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yarsan" class="mw-redirect" title="Yarsan">Yarsan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mandeanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandeanism">Mandeanism</a>, and in Iran, <a href="/wiki/Mithraism" title="Mithraism">Mithraism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manicheanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Manicheanism">Manicheanism</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá&#39;í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a>. Throughout its history the Middle East has been a major center of world affairs; a strategically, economically, politically, culturally, and religiously sensitive area.
</p><p>The world's earliest civilizations, Mesopotamia (<a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>, originated in the Fertile Crescent and <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> Valley regions of the ancient Near East. These were followed by the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urartian" class="mw-redirect" title="Urartian">Urartian</a> civilisations of <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> in pre-Iranian Persia, as well as the civilizations of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Levant" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Levant">Levant</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">Ebla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ugarit" title="Ugarit">Ugarit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aramea" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramea">Aramea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>), <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">Median</a> civilizations in Iran, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> (<a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>/Phoenicia) and the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> (<a href="/wiki/Majan_(civilization)" class="mw-redirect" title="Majan (civilization)">Magan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheba" title="Sheba">Sheba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iram_of_the_Pillars" title="Iram of the Pillars">Ubar</a>). The Near East was first largely unified under the <a href="/wiki/Neo_Assyrian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo Assyrian Empire">Neo Assyrian Empire</a>, then the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> followed later by the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Macedonian Empire">Macedonian Empire</a> and after this to some degree by the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">Iranian empires</a> (namely the <a href="/wiki/Arsacid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Arsacid Empire">Parthian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid Empires</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. However, it would be the later <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Arab Caliphates</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> which began with the Arab conquest of the region in the 7th century AD, that would first unify the entire Middle East as a distinct region and create the dominant <a href="/wiki/Islamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">ethnic identity</a> that largely (but not exclusively) persists today. The <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Kingdom of Armenia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">Seljuks</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavids</a>, the Ottoman Empire, and the British Empire also dominated the region.
</p><p>The modern Middle East began after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, when the Ottoman Empire, which was allied with the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a>, was defeated by the British Empire and their allies and <a href="/wiki/Partitioning_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Partitioning of the Ottoman Empire">partitioned</a> into a number of separate nations, initially under British and French Mandates. Other defining events in this transformation included the establishment of Israel in 1948 and the eventual departure of European powers, notably <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> by the end of the 1960s. They were supplanted in some part by the rising influence of the United States from the 1970s onwards.
</p><p>In the 20th century, the region's significant stocks of <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a> gave it new strategic and economic importance. Mass production of oil began around 1945, with Saudi Arabia, Iran, Kuwait, Iraq, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> having large quantities of oil.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Estimated <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves#Estimated_reserves_by_country" title="Oil reserves">oil reserves</a>, especially in Saudi Arabia and Iran, are some of the highest in the world, and the international oil cartel <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> is dominated by Middle Eastern countries.
</p><p>During the Cold War, the Middle East was a theater of ideological struggle between the two superpowers and their allies: <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> and the United States on one side, and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> on the other, as they competed to influence regional allies. Besides the political reasons there was also the "ideological conflict" between the two systems. Moreover, as <a href="/wiki/Louise_Fawcett" title="Louise Fawcett">Louise Fawcett</a> argues, among many important areas of contention, or perhaps more accurately of anxiety, were, first, the desires of the superpowers to gain strategic advantage in the region, second, the fact that the region contained some two thirds of the world's oil reserves in a context where oil was becoming increasingly vital to the economy of the Western world [...]<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Within this contextual framework, the United States sought to divert the Arab world from Soviet influence. Throughout the 20th and 21st centuries, the region has experienced both periods of relative peace and tolerance and periods of conflict particularly between <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunnis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shiite" class="mw-redirect" title="Shiite">Shiites</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_the_Middle_East" title="Demographics of the Middle East">Demographics of the Middle East</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Largest_metropolitan_areas_of_the_Middle_East" title="Largest metropolitan areas of the Middle East">Largest metropolitan areas of the Middle East</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_the_Middle_East" title="Ethnic groups in the Middle East">Ethnic groups in the Middle East</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> constitute the largest ethnic group in the Middle East, followed by <a href="/wiki/Turkic_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkic people">Turkic people</a>. Native ethnic groups of the region include, in addition to Arabs, <a href="/wiki/Arameans_in_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="Arameans in Syria">Arameans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baloch_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Baloch peoples">Baloch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copts" title="Copts">Copts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lurs" title="Lurs">Lurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandaeans" title="Mandaeans">Mandaeans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samaritans" title="Samaritans">Samaritans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shabak_people" title="Shabak people">Shabaks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tat_people_(Iran)" title="Tat people (Iran)">Tats</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zazas" title="Zazas">Zazas</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Middle_East&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small><i>(January 2015)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>"Migration has always provided an important vent for labor market pressures in the Middle East. For the period between the 1970s and 1990s, the Arab states of the PersianGulf in particular provided a rich source of employment for workers from Egypt, Yemen and the countries of the Levant, while Europe had attracted young workers from North African countries due both to proximity and the legacy of colonial ties between Franceand the majority of North African states." <sup id="cite_ref-Hassan_and_Dyer_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan_and_Dyer-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Migration" title="International Organization for Migration">International Organization for Migration</a>, there are 13 million first-generation migrants from <a href="/wiki/Arab_nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab nations">Arab nations</a> in the world, of which 5.8 reside in other Arab countries. Expatriates from Arab countries contribute to the circulation of financial and human capital in the region and thus significantly promote regional development. In 2009 Arab countries received a total of 35.1 billion USD in <a href="/wiki/Remittance" title="Remittance">remittance</a> in-flows and remittances sent to <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> from other Arab countries are 40 to 190 per cent higher than trade revenues between these and other Arab countries.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Civil_War" title="Somali Civil War">Somali Civil War</a> has greatly increased the size of the <a href="/wiki/Somali_diaspora" title="Somali diaspora">Somali diaspora</a>, as many of the best educated Somalis left for Middle Eastern countries as well as <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>.
</p><p>Non-Arab Middle Eastern countries such as <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> are also subject to important migration dynamics.
</p><p>A fair proportion of those migrating from Arab nations are from ethnic and religious minorities facing racial and or religious persecution and are not necessarily ethnic Arabs, Iranians or Turks.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Large numbers of <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish people">Kurds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jewish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish people">Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek people">Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Armenian people">Armenians</a> as well as many <a href="/wiki/Mandean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandean">Mandeans</a> have left nations such as Iraq, Iran, Syria and Turkey for these reasons during the last century. In Iran, many religious minorities such as <a href="/wiki/Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="Christians">Christians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baha%27i" class="mw-redirect" title="Baha&#39;i">Baha'is</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrians" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoroastrians">Zoroastrians</a> have left since the Islamic Revolution of 1979.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religions">Religions</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_Middle_East" title="Religion in the Middle East">Religion in the Middle East</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mosque.jpg/220px-Mosque.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mosque.jpg/330px-Mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mosque.jpg/440px-Mosque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Islam is the largest religion in the Middle East. Here, Muslim men are <a href="/wiki/Prostration" title="Prostration">prostrating</a> during prayer in a mosque.</div></div></div>
<p>The Middle East is very diverse when it comes to <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups" title="Major religious groups">religions</a>, many of which originated there. <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> is the largest religion in the Middle East, but other faiths that originated there, such as <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, are also well represented. Christians represent 40.5% of Lebanon, where the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Lebanon" title="President of Lebanon">Lebanese president</a>, half of the cabinet, and half of the parliament follow one of the various Lebanese Christian rites. There are also important minority religions like the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá&#39;í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yarsanism" title="Yarsanism">Yarsanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yazidis" title="Yazidis">Yazidism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandaeism" title="Mandaeism">Mandaeism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shabak_people#Religious_beliefs" title="Shabak people">Shabakism</a>, and in ancient times the region was home to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mesopotamian_religion" title="Ancient Mesopotamian religion">Mesopotamian religions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Canaanite_religion" title="Ancient Canaanite religion">Canaanite religions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mithraic_mysteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Mithraic mysteries">Mithraism</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Monotheist" class="mw-redirect" title="Monotheist">monotheist</a> <a href="/wiki/Gnostic" class="mw-redirect" title="Gnostic">gnostic</a> sects.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<p>The five top languages, in terms of numbers of speakers, are <a href="/wiki/Arabic_dialects" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic dialects">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">Kurdish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew languages">Hebrew</a>. Arabic and Hebrew represent the <a href="/wiki/Afro-Asiatic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Afro-Asiatic languages">Afro-Asiatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language family</a>. Persian and Kurdish belong to the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> language family. Turkish belongs to <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic</a> language family. About 20 minority languages are also spoken in the Middle East.
</p><p>Arabic, with all its dialects, are the most widely spoken languages in the Middle East, with <a href="/wiki/Literary_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Literary Arabic">Literary Arabic</a> being official in all North African and in most West Asian countries. Arabic dialects are also spoken in some adjacent areas in neighbouring Middle Eastern non-Arab countries. It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic branch</a> of the Afro-Asiatic languages. Several <a href="/wiki/Modern_South_Arabian_languages" title="Modern South Arabian languages">Modern South Arabian languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Mehri_language" title="Mehri language">Mehri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soqotri_language" title="Soqotri language">Soqotri</a> are also spoken Yemen and Oman. Another Semitic language such as <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a> and its dialects are spoken mainly by <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mandaeans" title="Mandaeans">Mandaeans</a>. There is also a <a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">Oasis Berber</a>-speaking community in Egypt where the language is also known as <a href="/wiki/Siwa_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Siwa language">Siwa</a>. It is a non-Semitic Afro-Asiatic language.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> is the second most spoken language. While it is primarily spoken in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and some border areas in neighbouring countries, the country is one of the region's largest and most populous. It belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian branch</a> of the family of <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>. Other Western Iranic languages spoken in the region include <a href="/wiki/Achomi_language" title="Achomi language">Achomi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daylami_language" title="Daylami language">Daylami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">Kurdish</a> dialects, <a href="/wiki/Semnani_language" title="Semnani language">Semmani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luri_language" title="Luri language">Lurish</a>, amongst many others.
</p><p>The third-most widely spoken language, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>, is largely confined to Turkey, which is also one of the region's largest and most populous countries, but it is present in areas in neighboring countries. It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic languages</a>, which have their origins in Central Asia. Another Turkic language, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azerbaijani</a>, is spoken by Azerbaijanis in Iran.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hebrew_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew languages">Hebrew</a> is one of the two official languages of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, the other being Arabic. Hebrew is spoken and used by over 80% of Israel's population, the other 20% using Arabic.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> is commonly taught and used as a second language, especially among the <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Upper_class" title="Upper class">upper classes</a>, in countries such as <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan" title="Kurdistan">Kurdistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> It is also a main language in some Emirates of the United Arab Emirates.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> is taught and used in many government facilities and media in <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, and is taught in some primary and secondary schools of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>. <a href="/wiki/Maltese_language" title="Maltese language">Maltese</a>, a Semitic language mainly spoken in Europe, is also used by the <a href="/wiki/Maltese_in_Egypt" title="Maltese in Egypt">Franco-Maltese diaspora</a> in Egypt.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Greek</a> speakers are also to be found in the region. <a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian</a> is spoken by the Georgian diaspora. <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> is spoken by a large portion of the Israeli population, because of <a href="/wiki/Aliyah_from_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States_in_the_1990s" class="mw-redirect" title="Aliyah from the Commonwealth of Independent States in the 1990s">emigration in the late 1990s</a>. Russian today is a popular unofficial language in use in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>; news, radio and sign boards can be found in Russian around the country after Hebrew and Arabic. <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Caucasian_languages" title="Northwest Caucasian languages">Circassian</a> is also spoken by the diaspora in the region and by almost all Circassians in Israel who speak Hebrew and English as well. The largest <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a>-speaking community in the Middle East is found in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, where as of  1995<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Middle_East&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> Romanian is spoken by 5% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> is widely spoken by migrant communities in many Middle Eastern countries, such as Saudi Arabia (where 20–25% of the population is South Asian), the United Arab Emirates (where 50–55% of the population is South Asian), and Qatar, which have large numbers of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladeshi</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> immigrants.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span>  <small><i>(December 2016)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Middle_East" title="Economy of the Middle East">Economy of the Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_East_economic_integration" title="Middle East economic integration">Middle East economic integration</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_and_Gas_Infrastructure_Persian_Gulf_(large).gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Oil_and_Gas_Infrastructure_Persian_Gulf_%28large%29.gif/300px-Oil_and_Gas_Infrastructure_Persian_Gulf_%28large%29.gif" width="300" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Oil_and_Gas_Infrastructure_Persian_Gulf_%28large%29.gif/450px-Oil_and_Gas_Infrastructure_Persian_Gulf_%28large%29.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Oil_and_Gas_Infrastructure_Persian_Gulf_%28large%29.gif/600px-Oil_and_Gas_Infrastructure_Persian_Gulf_%28large%29.gif 2x" data-file-width="959" data-file-height="844" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_and_Gas_Infrastructure_Persian_Gulf_(large).gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">Oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">gas</a> pipelines in the Middle-East</div></div></div>
<p>Middle Eastern economies range from being very poor (such as Gaza and Yemen) to extremely wealthy nations (such as Qatar and UAE). Overall, as of  2007<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Middle_East&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, according to the CIA World Factbook, all nations in the Middle East are maintaining a positive rate of growth.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>'s <i>World Development Indicators</i> database published on July 1, 2009, the three largest Middle Eastern economies in 2008 were Turkey ($794,228), Saudi Arabia ($467,601) and Iran ($385,143) in terms of <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">Nominal GDP</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WB-GDP-Nominal_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WB-GDP-Nominal-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Regarding nominal GDP per capita, the highest ranking countries are Qatar ($93,204), the UAE ($55,028), Kuwait ($45,920) and Cyprus ($32,745).<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Turkey ($1,028,897), Iran ($839,438) and Saudi Arabia ($589,531) had the largest economies in terms of <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP)">GDP-PPP</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WB-GDP-PPP_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WB-GDP-PPP-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> When it comes to per capita (PPP)-based income, the highest-ranking countries are Qatar ($86,008), Kuwait ($39,915), the UAE ($38,894), Bahrain ($34,662) and Cyprus ($29,853). The lowest-ranking country in the Middle East, in terms of per capita income (PPP), is the autonomous Palestinian Authority of Gaza and the West Bank ($1,100).
</p><p>The economic structure of Middle Eastern nations are different in the sense that while some nations are heavily dependent on export of only oil and oil-related products (such as Saudi Arabia, the UAE and Kuwait), others have a highly diverse economic base (such as Cyprus, Israel, Turkey and Egypt). Industries of the Middle Eastern region include oil and oil-related products, agriculture, cotton, cattle, dairy, textiles, leather products, surgical instruments, defence equipment (guns, ammunition, tanks, submarines, fighter jets, UAVs, and missiles). Banking is also an important sector of the economies, especially in the case of UAE and Bahrain.
</p><p>With the exception of Cyprus, Turkey, Egypt, Lebanon and Israel, tourism has been a relatively undeveloped area of the economy, in part because of the socially conservative nature of the region as well as political turmoil in certain regions of the Middle East. In recent years, however, countries such as the UAE, Bahrain, and Jordan have begun attracting greater number of tourists because of improving tourist facilities and the relaxing of tourism-related restrictive policies.
</p><p>Unemployment is notably high in the Middle East and North Africa region, particularly among young people aged 15–29, a demographic representing 30% of the region's total population. The total regional unemployment rate in 2005, according to the <a href="/wiki/International_Labour_Organization" title="International Labour Organization">International Labour Organization</a>, was 13.2%,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and among youth is as high as 25%,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> up to 37% in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> and 73% in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Italian, the expression "Vicino Oriente" (Near East) was also widely used to refer to Turkey, and <i>Estremo Oriente</i> (Far East or Extreme East) to refer to all of Asia east of Middle East</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the 1993 <i>Statistical Abstract of Israel</i> there were 250,000 Romanian speakers in Israel, at a population of 5,548,523 (census 1995).</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

"The expression Middle East is an old British label based on a British Western perception of the East divided into middle or near and far".
see also <cite class="citation web">Shohat, Ella. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070312062752/http://commposite.uqam.ca/videaz/docs/elshen.html">"Redrawing American Cartographies of Asia"</a>.
